  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_889c41d6-2cbd-419c-aad0-8ffa93c8cfe8'
      });
    }

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_673939361" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/673939361" />
    <a title="Jeffrey Keeten" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten"><img alt="Jeffrey Keeten" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1301087352p2/3427339.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/673939361?book_show_action=true">Jul 22, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeffrey Keeten" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeffrey Keeten" href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten">Jeffrey Keeten</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781611453270" href="/book/show/11056167-the-palace-of-dreams">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3427339-jeffrey-keeten?shelf=albanian">albanian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer673939361" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4019226821953715737"><b>”Mark-Alem pressed on, his mouth dry despite his attempts to reassure himself. After all, what did it really matter if he did get lost? He wasn’t on some vast plain or in a forest. He was merely inside the Palace. But still the thought of getting lost terrified him. How would he get through the night amid all these walls, these rooms, these cellars full of dreams and wild imaginings? He’d rather be on a frozen plain or in a forest infested with wolves. Yes, a thousand times rather!<br/><br/>He hurried on </b></span>
  <span id="freeText4019226821953715737" style="display:none"><b>”Mark-Alem pressed on, his mouth dry despite his attempts to reassure himself. After all, what did it really matter if he did get lost? He wasn’t on some vast plain or in a forest. He was merely inside the Palace. But still the thought of getting lost terrified him. How would he get through the night amid all these walls, these rooms, these cellars full of dreams and wild imaginings? He’d rather be on a frozen plain or in a forest infested with wolves. Yes, a thousand times rather!<br><br>He hurried on faster. How long had he been walking now? Suddenly he thought he hear a noise in the distance. Perhaps it’s only an illusion, he told himself. Then, after a little while, the sound of voices burst out again, more clearly this time, though he still couldn’t tell what direction it came from.<br><br>He went down another two or three steps and found himself in another corridor, which he deduced must be on the ground floor. The sound of voices faded for a few moments, then returned, nearer...Mark-Alem was practically running by now, his eyes fixed on the end of the corridor, where a faint square of light came in from outside. Please, God. let it be the back door!"</b><br><br><a href="http://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/EndlessHallway2_zpsea276092.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380430082i/862104.jpg" alt=" photo EndlessHallway2_zpsea276092.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><b>An empty, seemingly endless hallway can give a person a sense of disassociation.</b><br><br>There are no signs directing people in the proper directions at the Palace of Dreams. Mark-Alem finds himself lost not only in the corridors of the Palace, but also in the hour upon hour day to day work of selecting and interpreting dreams. He is descended from a prominent family called the Quprilis. They have contributed generations of powerful men to the Balkan Empire.<br><br><i>”For nearly four hundred years the Quprilis had seemed fated equally to glory and to misfortune. If its chronicles included great dignitaries, secretaries of state, governors, and prime ministers, they also told how just as many members of the family had been imprisoned or decapitated or had simply vanished.”</i><br><br>There are very few powerful families in the history of humanity that have not found themselves on the losing side of a power struggle at one point or at several points in history. After a few messy decapitations or quarterings these families eventually rise from the ashes (sometimes those ashes are relatives) and find that eventually the state has need of their services again. Now Mark-Alem’s mother is a Quprilis which means it is not evident immediately to the people he meets that he is related to that family. He is timid enough that he does not offer that information readily. Of course when he is summoned to the Palace of Dreams to be offered a position they are very aware of who he is.<br><br>He is assured he is the right sort of man.<br><br>Instead of starting at the bottom he starts in the middle of the hierarchy. <br><br>He moves up so quickly he barely has time to settle into one job before he is sent on to the next one. <br><br>Given the nature of the job which is to select dreams and interpret those dreams with the most important ones being sent to the Sultan to help him make decisions about the course of action he will take in running the empire you would think there would be a long and arduous training regime. There is not, at least not for Mark-Alem, but as the plot advances we start to get inklings that he is a pawn in a much bigger, much more dangerous game. <br><br>He is absolutely oblivious. <br><br>He is paranoid and nervous, but doesn’t know exactly what he should be paranoid and nervous about. <br><br>He is too worried about his workload and whether his interpretations of these dreams are correct. He wears out erasures writing what he thinks and then becoming paralyzed with doubt as to how his superiors would interpret his thoughts. Like any good bureaucrat he finds it is much safer to stifle any creativity and pass along the most bland, safest interpretations of the dreams he finds in his folder. Not that they need a reason to separate your head from your body, but certainly try not to hand it to them on a silver platter. <br><br>The empire is ruled by dreams. Every dream, no matter how mundane, is required to be written down by every citizen in the realm. I think it only seems reasonable that if I were to have a steamy dream say about my neighbor’s wife that I would make a few changes like say make it two horses in a pasture or really spice it up and have a pig with a goat. My luck somehow that would mean I was secretly plotting the downfall of the empire. These dreams are collected and hauled to the Palace of Dreams where they start the cycle of elimination of those dreams that are deemed worthless or fabricated (mine)and those that are thought to be important are pushed up the chain for further interpretation. As Mark-Alem wanders around his work, usually trying to find a door and usually on the wrong floor to find it, he discovers that sometimes the dreamers are brought in for further questioning about a dream they submitted. The questioning must be rigorous because sometimes those dreamers leave in a black coffin. You're not paranoid if actually there are reasons to be paranoid.<br><br>There is no sex in this book, barely a hint of desire. There is one moment where he passes a house where he knows two pretty sisters live and Mark-Alem might have felt a twitch or tingle, but other than that it seems as if the terror of his daily life is all consuming. There is talk at the end of the book of an arranged married, but Mark-Alem is about as interested in the details as he is in catalogued Elephant stool samples. <br><br>Ismail Kadare was in Albanian politics during the communist rule in the 1970s. He wrote a satirical poem in 1975 that came to the attention of the government and he was punished by not being able to publish for three years. In 1977 he publishes a book called <i>The Great Winter</i> that is flattering to Enver Hoxha, the Communist leader of Albania. Kadare later said that the book was the price of his freedom. In 1980 when the <i>Palace of Dreams</i> is published the book is immediately banned. Not a big surprise, dictatorships tend to not appreciate books that are Orwellian or Kafkaesque in nature. It seems to me that Kadare was fairly politically astute. He managed to be critical without getting himself killed. It also helps to be Albania’s most celebrated writer. In 1990 he applied for asylum in France. <br><br><a href="http://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/IsmailKadare_zpscc8f0dda.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380430082i/862105.jpg" alt=" photo IsmailKadare_zpscc8f0dda.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><b>Ismail Kadare: dissident against a dictatorship or did he collude to survive? Both I do believe and brilliantly in my opinion.</b><br><br>This book is the English translation of the French translation of the Albanian version. Yeah, I know, scary isn’t it? I don’t read Albanian and I unfortunately do not read French so I have no clue how much this story has been sifted and strained and blended and fluffed. I will say after I got over my initial shock at what the publishers had done to me, (I mean seriously the publisher couldn’t find an Albanian intellectual that has a solid command of English?)I found myself as nervous, paranoid, and as frustrated as Mark-Alem in trying to figure out what really was going on. This book is certainly a blatant condemnation of the Albanian government trying to control everything, granted they couldn’t figure out how to control their subject’s dreams, but if they could have they would. This is must read for those fans of Franz Kafka and George Orwell.<br><br>If you wish to see more of my most recent book and movie reviews, visit <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.jeffreykeeten.com">http://www.jeffreykeeten.com</a><br>I also have a Facebook blogger page at:<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten">https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="4019226821953715737" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_417798447" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/417798447" />
    <a title="Ema" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5746897-ema"><img alt="Ema" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1394635581p2/5746897.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/417798447?book_show_action=true">Sep 19, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ema" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ema" href="/user/show/5746897-ema">Ema</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099518273" href="/book/show/5978984-the-palace-of-dreams">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Ema by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/13135855-maia-oprea">Maia Oprea</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746897-ema?shelf=favs">favs</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746897-ema?shelf=bookish-gems">bookish-gems</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746897-ema?shelf=read-in-2012">read-in-2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746897-ema?shelf=magical-and-surreal">magical-and-surreal</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746897-ema?shelf=albania">albania</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer417798447" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6031523706552155971">I wonder why so few people have read this novel, because it's quite amazing. I can't say that it's completely original, because it reminded me of Kafka (<i>The Castle</i>) and Saramago (<i>All the Names</i>), but imagining an institution where people's dreams are analyzed... That is a brilliant idea, masterfully developed by Ismail Kadaré.<br/><br/>Mark-Alem comes from a powerful Albanian family, the Quiprili (Köprülü), and his relatives decide that he should apply for a job at one of the most influential institutions </span>
  <span id="freeText6031523706552155971" style="display:none">I wonder why so few people have read this novel, because it's quite amazing. I can't say that it's completely original, because it reminded me of Kafka (<i>The Castle</i>) and Saramago (<i>All the Names</i>), but imagining an institution where people's dreams are analyzed... That is a brilliant idea, masterfully developed by Ismail Kadaré.<br /><br />Mark-Alem comes from a powerful Albanian family, the Quiprili (Köprülü), and his relatives decide that he should apply for a job at one of the most influential institutions of the Ottoman Empire - Tabir Saray, the Palace of Dreams. Thus he begins his ascent to the top, although fearful and confused, never fully aware of what he is supposed to do. In this huge machinery of control, the dreams from all over the empire are gathered, sorted and analysed, in order to choose one Master Dream that is presented each Friday to the Sultan. Dreams are believed to foretell important political events, thus being of utmost importance to the Empire.<br /><br />We follow Mark-Alem's journey through the mysterious Palace of Dreams, with its nightmarish passages where he usually gets lost, with the thousands of dreams stacked away in its huge underground archive, with the kafkian beaurocracy and the strange happenings that make people paranoid. Without realising, Mark-Alem becomes an active part in the events that will unfold in the story, bringing misfortune to his family.<br /><br />Absorbed in the world of dreams, Mark-Alem comes to believe that this is the real world, powerful and vivid, while the reality outside gradually becomes gray, dull and less and less attractive. He gets more and more isolated, his relatives remaining his only connection to the earthly world. He seems oblivious to any romantic relationship and the only mention of a possible wife comes from his uncles, but we don't ever get to know the girl. The lack of a sexual dimension makes the character a bit too flat, but contributes to his total immersion in the fantastic world of dreams, a sort of hell that Ismail Kadaré wanted to create in his novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6031523706552155971" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2961881688" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2961881688" />
    <a title="Tsung" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4992851-tsung"><img alt="Tsung" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1298808605p2/4992851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2961881688?book_show_action=true">Sep 13, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tsung" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tsung" href="/user/show/4992851-tsung">Tsung</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2961881688" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17329423694856707145">This is another fascinating story from Ismail Kadare. It is a story of the Quprilis (Turkish: Koprulu), a noble Albanian family, and the Tabir Sarrail, the Palace of Dreams, a government ministry which wields great unbridled power. It reads very much like magical realism but is set against a historical backdrop.<br/><br/>Set in the time of the Ottoman Empire, the influential and respected Quprili family had illustrious members who were Viziers and other important officials. Not so for Mark-Alem who was ra</span>
  <span id="freeText17329423694856707145" style="display:none">This is another fascinating story from Ismail Kadare. It is a story of the Quprilis (Turkish: Koprulu), a noble Albanian family, and the Tabir Sarrail, the Palace of Dreams, a government ministry which wields great unbridled power. It reads very much like magical realism but is set against a historical backdrop.<br /><br />Set in the time of the Ottoman Empire, the influential and respected Quprili family had illustrious members who were Viziers and other important officials. Not so for Mark-Alem who was rather insignificant, without much talent or gumption. What he did have was a maternal Quprili inheritance but counterbalanced by an Arabic paternal descent. He is thrust into a highly sought after career at the Tabir Sarrail. <br /><br />The task of the Tabir is to classify and examine the dreams of all citizens without exception. The premise is that Allah sends a forewarning to the world through dreams which may come from anybody. The Tabir has to sift out ordinary dreams, as well as the “fake news” dreams, leaving only the potentially important ones and to interpret these selected dreams. Distilled at the end are Master-Dreams which determine the future of the empire.<br /><br />The Tabir is a labyrinth of hallways and rooms, filled with thousands of nameless staff who stick to fixed routines and run like clockwork. It is in this Orwellian environment that the addlepated Mark-Alem has to, not only survive, but to thrive. That they collect, sort and analyse dreams seems rather odd and could only be allegorical. Also symbolic is how Mar-Alem keeps losing his way in the Tabir.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The story is like a game of chess. With gambits and sacrifices from both sides of the board. It becomes more obvious later that the push for Mark-Alem is politically motivated. Like on a chess board, he starts as a lowly pawn from the bottom and gets slowly advanced all the way to the back row to convert to a more important chess piece.<br /><br />There is nothing endearing about Mark-Alem but one feels sorry for him as he gets swept along by politics. While he had no outstanding ability or wisdom, he had insight into his situation. Even at the pinnacle of his career, he still understood that it could all be taken away just as easily as it came. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Intriguing. A nice way to end my binge on Kadare.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17329423694856707145" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_109750606" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/109750606" />
    <a title="john Adams" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5624-john-adams"><img alt="john Adams" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1241329443p2/5624.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/109750606?book_show_action=true">Jul 02, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="john Adams" class="user" itemprop="url" name="john Adams" href="/user/show/5624-john-adams">john Adams</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5624-john-adams?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer109750606" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1916004794807537529">The Palace of Dream, by Ismail Kadare<br/><br/>If Kafka's The Castle and Orwell's 1984 got freaky with it and had a baby the result would be Kadare's The Palace of Dreams. Karade is an Albanian and I would argue that the Palace of Dreams belongs to the long and productive tradition of subversive communist literature that cleverly disguises its critique in a novel about the fantastic. Karade's subversion isn't so disguised; it kind of hits in the face, but he's Albanian and not a Russian and he lives in Fr</span>
  <span id="freeText1916004794807537529" style="display:none">The Palace of Dream, by Ismail Kadare<br /><br />If Kafka's The Castle and Orwell's 1984 got freaky with it and had a baby the result would be Kadare's The Palace of Dreams. Karade is an Albanian and I would argue that the Palace of Dreams belongs to the long and productive tradition of subversive communist literature that cleverly disguises its critique in a novel about the fantastic. Karade's subversion isn't so disguised; it kind of hits in the face, but he's Albanian and not a Russian and he lives in France, but the novel is of this type. The Palace of Dreams is a monolithic government agency that feels like it comes out of Orwell or the movie Brazil. The agency's mission is to gather and interpret the dreams of all the citizens. The protagonist, Mark-Alem, gets assigned to a mid-level position in Selection. The job of Selection is to to choose the dreams that are worthy of Interpretation from those that are garbage. Mark-Alem starts at a mid-level position because his family is second in the land only to the Sultan (the book is set in the Ottoman Empire, but this time period is of little consequence). The Palace itself feels like Kafka's The Castle. It is a labyrinth in which Mark-Alem is constantly lost and he winds up turning corners to find himself face to face with busy bureaucrats and big desks. As he spends his time in Selection more of the Palace is revealed. It is by design mysterious. Everyone works at their position but few know to what end. It is revealed to him that there is a Master Dream that is of interest to the Sultan. The Master Dream has something to do with major political events, such as assassinations and wars and has existed and interpreted for hundreds of years. After more of the Palace is revealed, Mark-Alem goes to work one day and finds himself promoted into the division of Interpretation. In Interpretation he reads several dreams and in particular one he choose earlier from Selection to be interpreted. Little of the dream is discussed, but it has to do with a bridge, a raging bull, and a fire. In a nut shell, the story follows the life of Mark-Alem as a bureaucrat in the Palace. He works, he has coffee breaks, he files papers, and he discusses his work with his family (despite the fact that he is supposed to be secretive). Underlying the Palace and the prose is a tension. Everyone works, but because they do not know to what end and because they are forced to be secretive, everyone is paranoid and second guesses their selections and interpretations. Then one day it all changes. His family is raided by the secret Police. Their servants are killed and his Uncle is arrested. Mark-Alem feels his family may know why, but he is left in the dark. He goes to work the next day and the Palace is a buzz in whispered gossip. The bureaucrats all talk of what happened to Mark-Alem's family. The next day there is retaliation. Mark-Alem's family, second in the nation, has retaliated in assignations and political moves. Mark-Alem finds him again promoted, but his time into a directors position. As a director he has the access to research what has happened. He discovers that the dream he Selected and Interpreted was a part of the Master Dream and predicted a power struggle between his family and the Sultan. His family would gain power at the cost of his Uncle's life. Mark-Alem wonders if he were planted by his family to Select and Interpret the Master Dream. He wonders if it was a coincidence that he is now a man in power or that if he were set up to be handed the Master Dream and raised to Interpret it as such. He wonders and he waits, for he knows one day the secret police will come and take him. Until that time he continues to work.<br /><br />Ismail Karade said that he wanted to create “vision of hell.” I would say that he succeeded. Mark-Alem's life is directionless, mundane, and worst all he waits in ignorance of a when horrible future will catch up to him. I think that having the knowledge that the future will be 'bad' but not knowing when or how is one of the worst feelings of all time. It creates anticipation and expectation and these feelings are often more powerful than the reality itself. At the same time, Mark-Alem's life is mundane and pointless. He is not waiting for death by living an exciting fun life, he is waiting for death by barely getting by in isolation and paranoia. It is a hell, a very modern and distinct vision. The only critique I could offer is that this reality of hell is present, but that the emotion behind those feelings isn't well captured. The book is not character driven, but setting driven. But I don't consider this a bad thing, but a good choice. The setting, The Palace of Dreams is ultra cool. The prose is quick and fun. Together those elements make this a fun book to read. The result is that Karade captures a modern hell in a fun way, and that makes this a really great book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1916004794807537529" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1108164004" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1108164004" />
    <a title="Jane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7177008-jane"><img alt="Jane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1388366904p2/7177008.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1108164004?book_show_action=true">Jun 08, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jane" href="/user/show/7177008-jane">Jane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099518273" href="/book/show/5978984-the-palace-of-dreams">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7177008-jane?shelf=albania">albania</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7177008-jane?shelf=owned-books">owned-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7177008-jane?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7177008-jane?shelf=dystopia">dystopia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1108164004" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer599563609465768637">Kadare's metaphor for a monolithic police state and its workings. Set in the late 19th century Ottoman Empire--I figured this out from several subtle hints in the novel--along with elements from the late 20th century, this novel tells of a young man, Mark Alem, who is employed by the Palace of Dreams, the author's surreal intelligence agency, where dreams from all over the empire are collected, sorted, interpreted, with an eye to discovering which might be a "Master-Dream" pointing to a possible</span>
  <span id="freeText599563609465768637" style="display:none">Kadare's metaphor for a monolithic police state and its workings. Set in the late 19th century Ottoman Empire--I figured this out from several subtle hints in the novel--along with elements from the late 20th century, this novel tells of a young man, Mark Alem, who is employed by the Palace of Dreams, the author's surreal intelligence agency, where dreams from all over the empire are collected, sorted, interpreted, with an eye to discovering which might be a "Master-Dream" pointing to a possible coup or other upheaval in the State. When one is discovered, the sultan's secret police can nip a possible plot in the bud and do away with any perpetrators. Mark Alem starts out in the Selection Department and passes along a file containing what he feels might be a possibly incriminating dream: a wasteland filled with garbage, a musical instrument, a rampaging bull, and a bridge. When he is promoted to the Interpretation Section, he is faced with the very same dream. We don't know his final interpretation, but agents from the Master Dream Section become very busy....<br /><br />A chilling and nightmarish novel, reminiscent of Kafka--the claustrophobic, labyrinthine corridors of the Palace are evoked frighteningly. Mark-Alem must find his way from one department to another alone, hoping for help. On his day off, he notices how pale and insipid the real world has become as compared with the inner lives of people in the Palace. Atmospheric. <br /><br />Very highly recommended. I'd advise reading the author's Three-Arched Bridge first if possible to get some backstory.</span>
  <a data-text-id="599563609465768637" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1512585854" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1512585854" />
    <a title="Blair" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/272909-blair"><img alt="Blair" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1493499323p2/272909.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1512585854?book_show_action=true">Jan 16, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Blair" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Blair" href="/user/show/272909-blair">Blair</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099518273" href="/book/show/5978984-the-palace-of-dreams">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=sff">sff</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=1980s-release">1980s-release</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=1990s-release">1990s-release</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=translated">translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=read-on-kindle">read-on-kindle</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1512585854" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14497625666156331624">The unique idea at the heart of this story is instantly intriguing. Mark-Alem, scion of the powerful Quprili family, is given a job at a prestigious institution: the Tabir Sarrail, or Palace of Dreams. Transcriptions of citizens' dreams are collected here in their thousands, then pored over, analysed and interpreted for indications that they contain some divine prediction, a message of glory (or doom) for the Empire. The eventual aim of this mammoth task is to identify the 'Master-Dream', the mo</span>
  <span id="freeText14497625666156331624" style="display:none">The unique idea at the heart of this story is instantly intriguing. Mark-Alem, scion of the powerful Quprili family, is given a job at a prestigious institution: the Tabir Sarrail, or Palace of Dreams. Transcriptions of citizens' dreams are collected here in their thousands, then pored over, analysed and interpreted for indications that they contain some divine prediction, a message of glory (or doom) for the Empire. The eventual aim of this mammoth task is to identify the 'Master-Dream', the most meaningful and portentous of them all, which is delivered to the Sultan on a weekly basis. <br /><br />Unsurprisingly, the novel has often been compared to the works of Orwell and Kafka. Mark-Alem's job is bureaucratic yet bizarre, and cloaked in so much mystery that at first, he doesn't even know what he's supposed to be doing, or the way around the vast Palace, or what all the oddly-named departments do. There are recurring scenes in which he wanders the corridors, lost and disorientated. Parallels are drawn between being swallowed up by this place and the experience of sleep - or even death. Having become accustomed to its strange ways, Mark-Alem finds real life comparatively insipid: 'the whole world seemed to have lost all its colour, as if after a long illness... How tedious, grasping and confined this world seemed in comparison with the one he now served!' Yet when he's at work, the dream transcripts often seem incomprehensible to him. At times he marks them at random, and it's this cavalier approach to the task that ultimately brings about the plot's bloody climax. Its meaning as a political allegory is clear, but the novel is always equally enjoyable as an imaginative (often quite suspenseful) story.<br /><br />Had this been a smoother read, my rating would be higher, as I really liked the story. However, I thought it had a stilted and awkward feel all the way through, and I'm convinced this can only be the result of it having been translated twice - this English version is not translated directly from the original Albanian, but from the the French edition. There were a couple of unusual recurring phrases that really jarred, and seemed like inaccurate choices; certain words were repeated with irritating frequency. I found all of this really offputting and I'm afraid it also makes me less likely to read more Kadare (though I'd first need to establish whether all of them have been through the weird Albanian-French-English treatment).</span>
  <a data-text-id="14497625666156331624" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/576293760?book_show_action=true">Mar 31, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Louise" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Louise" href="/user/show/9281934-louise">Louise</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9281934-louise?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer576293760" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1390785751396309812">In this spare novel Ismail Kadare creates a metaphor for the police state. A young distaff scion of a family powerful enough to rival the leaders of Ottoman Empire is given a job in the Palace of Dreams. Here, a huge machinery gathers the dreams from around the Empire. It copies, sorts, interprets, sifts and archives them.<br/><br/>Just as a thought police thrives on rumor and innuendo, so does the Palace. The power struggles of the mighty, are not discussed or understood even among the intimates of the p</span>
  <span id="freeText1390785751396309812" style="display:none">In this spare novel Ismail Kadare creates a metaphor for the police state. A young distaff scion of a family powerful enough to rival the leaders of Ottoman Empire is given a job in the Palace of Dreams. Here, a huge machinery gathers the dreams from around the Empire. It copies, sorts, interprets, sifts and archives them.<br /><br />Just as a thought police thrives on rumor and innuendo, so does the Palace. The power struggles of the mighty, are not discussed or understood even among the intimates of the participants, but are palpable throughout the empire and very keenly felt at the Palace of Dreams. Kadare demonstrates how the fear of the unknown paralyzes bystanders and how participants keep everyone in the dark.<br /><br />While this book is over 15 years old, I had not heard of it or its author. I found it through an Amazon reviewer whose interests run parallel to mine. This book (and perhaps the author-I'll have to read more Kadare) should be on academic reading lists along with the works of George Orwell and Aldous Huxley.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1390785751396309812" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_404892020" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/404892020" />
    <a title="Ruqaiya Said" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2636318-ruqaiya-said"><img alt="Ruqaiya Said" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1325133571p2/2636318.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/404892020?book_show_action=true">Sep 01, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ruqaiya Said" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ruqaiya Said" href="/user/show/2636318-ruqaiya-said">Ruqaiya Said</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Noor al zubaidy</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Ruqaiya by: </span>
      Nema al Araby
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2636318-ruqaiya-said?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2636318-ruqaiya-said?shelf=extraordinary">extraordinary</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer404892020" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3443400799704634234">I put this book down in complete awe. I remember feeling the same when I put down Chronicle in Stone. Kadare is an amazing writer. The Palace of Dreams like most of Kadare's work is political. It talks about the "Tabir Sarail", a secret government agency under the watchful eyes of a totalitarian government that specializes in analyzing dreams of the citizens. The main character, Mark- Alem belongs to the influential Quprili family who have had their share of political trials and tribulations ove</span>
  <span id="freeText3443400799704634234" style="display:none">I put this book down in complete awe. I remember feeling the same when I put down Chronicle in Stone. Kadare is an amazing writer. The Palace of Dreams like most of Kadare's work is political. It talks about the "Tabir Sarail", a secret government agency under the watchful eyes of a totalitarian government that specializes in analyzing dreams of the citizens. The main character, Mark- Alem belongs to the influential Quprili family who have had their share of political trials and tribulations over the yeas. He is however not in the least bit interested in pursuing a political career. Ironically, he is sucked into the very political whirlpool he has always avoided and that his mother sought to protect him from over the years.<br />Don't be fooled by the synopsis of the book, this is no fast paced thriller. Yet, Kadare manages to keep you interested the whole time with the calm narrative,mellifluous prose and of course what he does with the topic of dream analysis and interpretation. How he does this, I have absolutely no idea. The man is a genius,and although this might be impetuous of me to say considering I haven't read ALL his work, why on earth hasn't he received the Nobel prize yet??!!!<br /><br />Some of my favorite quotes are:<br /><br />"Yes, of course there were risks, but they were of dream dangers in a world of dreams- the very world the Ancients used to wish to be transported to when they were in trouble and cried," Oh God, let it be only a dream"<br /><br />"In those files was all the sleep in the world, an ocean of terror on the vast surface of which they tried to find some tiny signs or signals. Hapless wretches that we are!" <br /><br />"Aren't sleep and death close neighbors?"<br /><br />" It was as if dreams no longer dared visit him, knowing he'd fathomed their secrets and could tell them to go and find someone else to play their tricks on."<br /><br />"What had happened, then, to life, to mankind, to everything here below? There- he smiled inwardly as if at some precious secret- there, in his files, all was so different, so beautiful, so full of imagination... The colors of the clouds, the trees,the snow, the bridges, the chimneys, the birds- all were so much more vivid and strong. And the movement of people and things was freer and more graceful, like stags running the mist, defying the laws of space and time! How tedious,grasping , and confined this world seemed in comparison with the one he now served!"<br /><br />"There before him lay the melancholy aggregate of the sleep of one of the vastest empires in the world: more than forty nationalities, representatives of almost all religions and of every race. if the report had included the whole globe, it wouldn't have made much difference. To all intents and purposes it covered the sleep of the entire planet- terrible and infinite shadows, a bottomless abyss from which Mark- Alem was trying to dredge up a few fragments of truth. Hypnos himself, the Greek god of sleep, couldn't have known more than he did about dreams."<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3443400799704634234" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_124658543" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/124658543" />
    <a title="Caitlin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4314352-caitlin"><img alt="Caitlin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1284996890p2/4314352.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/124658543?book_show_action=true">Oct 04, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Caitlin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Caitlin" href="/user/show/4314352-caitlin">Caitlin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer124658543" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15860398389704021664">This dystopic novel in some ways works much better than George Orwell's 1984, which had Gregory Zamyatin's "We" as its predecessor. It is a more original work than 1984 and is delightfully written. A powerful and important critique of totalitarian regimes. Kadare, an Albanian, wrote the Palace of Dreams as a critique of Tito's Yugoslavia, but it can be applied to all hegemonic states.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_63147987" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/63147987" />
    <a title="Alta" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2404463-alta"><img alt="Alta" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1419108364p2/2404463.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/63147987?book_show_action=true">Jul 12, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alta" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alta" href="/user/show/2404463-alta">Alta</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer63147987" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8776508241067111753">The Palace of Dreams, written in Tirana between 1976 and 1981, takes us into an entirely different universe set at the fictitious crossroads of a 20th century dictatorship and the 14th century Ottoman Empire. Characters from those ancient times mix with contemporary characters—state employees and office clerks reminiscent of Kafka’s world—in a bureaucratic labyrinth identical to any other bureaucracy, save for its purpose: to collect, sort, interpret and finally choose the “Master-dream” of all </span>
  <span id="freeText8776508241067111753" style="display:none">The Palace of Dreams, written in Tirana between 1976 and 1981, takes us into an entirely different universe set at the fictitious crossroads of a 20th century dictatorship and the 14th century Ottoman Empire. Characters from those ancient times mix with contemporary characters—state employees and office clerks reminiscent of Kafka’s world—in a bureaucratic labyrinth identical to any other bureaucracy, save for its purpose: to collect, sort, interpret and finally choose the “Master-dream” of all the dreams dreamt throughout the Empire, and to decipher in it the fate of the Empire and of its rulers. <br /><br />The Palace of Dreams incorporates the traits of all powerful secret institutions—one cannot help think of the Sigurimi, the Albanian Secret Police of the Communist era—as well as the characteristics of an almost Totemic figure, a Kafkaesque Castle whose rules no one can figure out. Kadare himself has declared that this is probably his best novel from a literary standpoint, and very likely his most courageous, an opinion the Albanian Communist regime must have agreed with, considering that shortly after its release the novel was banned. <br /><br />But Kadare’s genius is such that, in the end, the Palace of Dreams has no precise signification, except that revealed by its name. It is a fabulous, otherworldly place where the “real world” doesn’t exist, sleep is reality’s only substance, and it isn’t the real, as we know from Freud, that brings the dream into being, but the other way around. Thus, at the end of the novel, one of the dreams that the main character, Mark-Alem Quprili, who works at the Palace, sorted and filed at the beginning of the novel, makes an unexpected appearance, literally acting upon the present and causing the drama the reader has been anticipating all along. <br /><br /><br /> <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8776508241067111753" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2654546785" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2654546785" />
    <a title="Ila" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56761305-ila"><img alt="Ila" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546267699p2/56761305.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2654546785?book_show_action=true">Jan 13, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ila" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ila" href="/user/show/56761305-ila">Ila</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56761305-ila?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2654546785" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3399140459117239698">“And why, in your opinion, is the Palace of Dreams so formidable?” asked Mark-Alem’s mother.<br/><br/>“It isn’t so in the way you may suppose,” said Kurt, with a covert glance at his nephew. “I was thinking of something else. If you ask me, of all the mechanisms of State, the Palace of Dreams is the most remote from human will. Do you see what I mean? It’s the most impersonal, the blindest, the most deadly, and so the most autocratic.”<br/><br/>The very idea that something as private, subconscious and subjective as</span>
  <span id="freeText3399140459117239698" style="display:none">“And why, in your opinion, is the Palace of Dreams so formidable?” asked Mark-Alem’s mother.<br /><br />“It isn’t so in the way you may suppose,” said Kurt, with a covert glance at his nephew. “I was thinking of something else. If you ask me, of all the mechanisms of State, the Palace of Dreams is the most remote from human will. Do you see what I mean? It’s the most impersonal, the blindest, the most deadly, and so the most autocratic.”<br /><br />The very idea that something as private, subconscious and subjective as dreams are analysed and interpreted via bureaucracy is eerie, monstrous even. I drew comparisons other than Stalinist Albania and Ottoman Albania with the Spanish Inquisition. Anonymous informers, rival parties with simmering discontent coming to a head giving rise to a climate of paranoia, confusion, helplessness is common to many totalitarian States. Control is key to their subsistence. But the rather fanatical and extreme reliance on dream analysis also evokes an "idealistic" police state. <br /><br />As with Kadare's novels, there are multiple layers and allegories. Dream can be considered as a symbol of artistic creativity, since it is often unconscious impulses, longings, and abstract theories that translate into material for dreams. Censoring, parsing, butchering artistic license as happened in Hoxha's Albania is possibly one meaning Kadare wanted to convey although, as I am not an Albanian, I am aware I am missing a number of culturally significant meanings.<br /><br />Reality is often questionable here as every part of dream is linked to some aspect of government. Mark-Alem accustomed to wander in the ethereal and shadowy world of dreams finds the return to his immediate reality jarring. Work in such a place seems infernal to him, it saps him of his vitality. Luckily I read Dante's Inferno before this and so can correlate with the Palace of Dreams and Hell.<br />Unfortunately for the Koprulus to survive, he must sacrifice his all. He is the man immured in the bridge. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3399140459117239698" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63497622-ioana-ioana?shelf=up-close-and-personal">up-close-and-personal</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2970384465&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2970384465" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10980302631347185889"><b>1981, Dreaming the future</b><br/><br/>As labyrinthine as Jaques Tati’s <i>Playtime</i> scenery, rivaling in its ability to weave lore into history with Rushdie, Kadare’s <i>Palace of Dreams</i> was an absolutely gut wrenching, devastating read, rife with melancholy, numbness and fatalism. <br/><br/>In lucid dreaming, this one technique calls for you to <i>question reality</i>. Or at least its nature. To ask yourself, from time to time during wakefulness, if you are in the confinements of the real world and take a quick test. The catch is </span>
  <span id="freeText10980302631347185889" style="display:none"><b>1981, Dreaming the future</b><br /><br />As labyrinthine as Jaques Tati’s <i>Playtime</i> scenery, rivaling in its ability to weave lore into history with Rushdie, Kadare’s <i>Palace of Dreams</i> was an absolutely gut wrenching, devastating read, rife with melancholy, numbness and fatalism. <br /><br />In lucid dreaming, this one technique calls for you to <i>question reality</i>. Or at least its nature. To ask yourself, from time to time during wakefulness, if you are in the confinements of the real world and take a quick test. The catch is that, if done right, you may succeed in conjuring the habit during sleep and take control of your dreams. But at what point doubt becomes too much doubt?<br />Our anti-hero, Mark-Alem performs the opposite of a reality check - the harder he attempts to attribute signs of the future to the conscripted dreams, the more light is drained from the picture of the real world. He’s absolutely depressed, demolished, splintered - which is easily recognized by the universal ugliness he missed before but now splurges over, leaving the world insignificant and meaningless.<br /><br />Behind the doors of a blue palace, the dreamers blood is shed. <br /><br /></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_444233763" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/444233763" />
    <a title="Helen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/156067-helen"><img alt="Helen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1189540331p2/156067.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/444233763?book_show_action=true">Oct 29, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Helen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Helen" href="/user/show/156067-helen">Helen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099518273" href="/book/show/5978984-the-palace-of-dreams">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/156067-helen?shelf=dystopian">dystopian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/156067-helen?shelf=literary">literary</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer444233763&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer444233763" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3488944352627779376">The premise of this novel is wonderful and raises all sorts of possibilities that are not touched on. I'm reviewing it without reading other reviews (I feel like I've missed the big picture somehow, as the last chapter simply left me confused and unfulfilled), but I do see that it's meant to be political. What a different and interesting book this could be from a psychological angle or a explorative dystopian angle ("this is why our society works this way"). There's no doubt of Kadare's talent a</span>
  <span id="freeText3488944352627779376" style="display:none">The premise of this novel is wonderful and raises all sorts of possibilities that are not touched on. I'm reviewing it without reading other reviews (I feel like I've missed the big picture somehow, as the last chapter simply left me confused and unfulfilled), but I do see that it's meant to be political. What a different and interesting book this could be from a psychological angle or a explorative dystopian angle ("this is why our society works this way"). There's no doubt of Kadare's talent as a writer. This quote, in particular, was great:<br /><br />"In the same way as a plant or a fruit remains under the earth for a while before appearing above ground, so men's dreams were now buried in sleep; but it didn't follow that this would always be so. One day dreams would emerge into the light of day and take their rightful place in human thought, experience and action."<br /><br />I assumed Mark-Alem was quite young (age 20?) until it was revealed at the end that he is 28. Mark-Alem is almost disappointed to learn that Tabir has humble beginnings, dreams flowing in from hovels and villages, but is relieved to have it restored to his previous impressions of grandeur. He is again disappointed by the plain instruments of the Albanian rhapsodists (minstrels?), thinking that his family's epic should be honored by some elaborate musical contraptions. It's hard to tell why he is characterized this way -- though he enjoys power and status when he has it, one does not take him for a social climber. This is the first Kadare book I've read, so perhaps it is Kadare coming through, not his character.<br /><br />Kadare deftly captures Mark-Alem's general mindset with his writing style -- the Tabir is described as if by a person in a dream: nothing seems certain, things are confusing and terrifying. Halls stretch on forever, directions are muddled and disorienting, people seem grotesque...they also appear and disappear. The characters Mark-Alem encounters are sadly one-dimensional (and maybe even all too similar), but perhaps this reflects the theme of superficiality in this novel, as well as the nightmarish quality of the Tabir.<br /><br />If I had to choose one word to describe Mark-Alem, particularly in the first chapters of the novel, I'd choose "wishy-washy" -- he became less like this as the novel went on (though he still drifted through life, barely taking anything in...hardly a character of any agency whatsoever), so I can't attribute the wishy-washiness to Kadare's writing. Allow me to paraphrase his thinking processes at work: "I don't know how to do this! Let me do it like this! No, let me not! No, never mind, first way was right! People are probably watching me go cuckoo. I can't ask for help! Argh, what do I do?!" (Perhaps this is the general state of mind for someone living under this type of government?) There were also numerous moments when Kadare wrote that X was one way, but simultaneously it was also the opposite or something different. Or that Mark thought he should do X, but then perhaps he shouldn't. I wanted Kadare, or Mark-Alem at least, to make up his mind. GRRR. Frustrating!<br /><br />Perhaps this is typical of a society that lives under totalitarian rule, accepting that one has no idea what's going on and not thinking to ask questions, but Mark-Alem is certainly naive going into his job, armed with only rumors. He slowly learns about the Tabir in bits and chunks over coffee breaks. Totally mysterious. (Why does he only learn info during morning coffee breaks -- no afternoon break, no lunch?) To me the sense of mystery was too overbearing. Instead of creating suspense, it just seemed like Kadare didn't know the details himself, so he only glossed over them. It's telling that the institution's arms operate blindly. Each branch only knows its own business and rarely seems to wonder about other branches' work. There's also a great emphasis on status throughout the book: the Q family name, Mark-Alem as a n00b, and then how he interacts with people who are above and below his standing. I wished that he would come across as less weak and passive.<br /><br />Why was he chosen for Selection, and then subsequently, Interpretation and Master Dreams, and then to be assistant to the Director? I assume it was due to his family name or thanks to the other "agents" the Q family has working at the Tabir, but there doesn't seem to be any rhyme or reason otherwise. Why would a state institution want a Q as one of their employees, particularly higher up, if they see the Q family as their enemy? Is it to show the Qs' ultimate takeover of the government and their family status? I am as lost as Mark-Alem was in that labyrinthine building that held the Tabir.<br /><br />Despite no recommendations being allowed at the Tabir, why was Mark-Alem accepted as an employee when he clearly came in with a recommendation? Considering his family name, weren't they suspicious of him and his intents? How does he prevent himself from getting influenced by outside forces anyway? He still lives with his family; he still interacts with his oh-so-powerful (and apparently traitorous to the state) uncles.<br /><br />Why do people willingly submit dreams (for that chance to be "rewarded")? I'm sure it's partly due to the political pressure, but we also learn that some parts of the empire are sleepless/dreamless. How does the government know people are actually submitting everything they dream? How can they be sure the people actually recall their dreams to accurate detail (which doesn't happen in the real world)? The other methods that Tabir has, theoretically, can''t they work for everyone's dreams? Why this fussy method of reporting dreams and having them transported by couriers? (Maybe I'm expecting a more futuristic book...) If this had been a sci-fi novel, it would have been so different.<br /><br />Why do states like season, weather, temperature affect dreaming? What about the evil dream that spooked the horses of the couriers that deliver dreams? Kadare touched upon so many things that might have been interesting to delve into, but alas, we only skim the surface of this unusual world.<br /><br />The novel also reminds me of the movie <i>Paycheck</i> in that the Sovereign uses the weekly Master Dream as something of an omen, to predict triumphs and crises, thus pursuing them to the anticipated end. In <i>Paycheck</i>, the government harnesses the technology to predict the future and then essentially ACTS to make that future come true (ex. predictive machine says there will be war with X country, so the government strikes X country *first* as a preemptive measure, thus starting the war). Without knowing that future possibility of war, the war might not have started. I don't know if the Philip K. Dick story that <i>Paycheck</i> was based on also contains the same point -- perhaps it does.<br /><br />Finally, the idea of reducing dreamstuff, which is normally something light and fun, into drudgery for thousands of government desk drones, is interesting. In some ways it reminds me of Terry Gilliam's film <i>Brazil</i>. It also solidifies in me that I could never be happy as a desk drone.<br /><br />Certainly it was a book with many layers to peel away (I could probably continue writing at length on it and do a whole essay), but I still can't help but feel that its potential was mostly wasted. SO MUCH to explore in a society where the government is privy to its citizens' dreams. So much. I thought the prose was masterful but need to demote it by one star for all the story that I feel is left untold. Naturally Kadare wrote the book/story he wanted to write, and that's what's important -- but I really did have loftier expectations for such a thought-provoking premise going in.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3488944352627779376" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_57551305" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/57551305" />
    <a title="Brad" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1022982-brad"><img alt="Brad" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442484575p2/1022982.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/57551305?book_show_action=true">May 27, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brad" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brad" href="/user/show/1022982-brad">Brad</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=personal-mythology">personal-mythology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=hyperreality">hyperreality</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=unfinished">unfinished</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=paused">paused</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer57551305" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17438490969362339158">Sadly, I wasn't able to finish <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/267147.Dream_Palace_of_the_Arabs_A_Generation_s_Odyssey' title='Dream Palace of the Arabs A Generation's Odyssey by Fouad Ajami' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Palace of Dreams</a>. I lost it with only thirty-eight pages left, which was an oddly fitting end to what turned out to be an odd experience with an odd book. <br/><br/>The book is about a mysterious ministry in the Ottoman Empire that collects, reads, sorts and interprets the dreams of its citizens as a prophetic means of unlocking crimes and conspiracies against the state. Into this organization goes Mark-Alem, a maternal son of the powerful Quprili family.<br/><br/>I reached a plac</span>
  <span id="freeText17438490969362339158" style="display:none">Sadly, I wasn't able to finish <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/267147.Dream_Palace_of_the_Arabs_A_Generation_s_Odyssey" title="Dream Palace of the Arabs A Generation's Odyssey by Fouad Ajami" rel="nofollow noopener">The Palace of Dreams</a>. I lost it with only thirty-eight pages left, which was an oddly fitting end to what turned out to be an odd experience with an odd book. <br /><br />The book is about a mysterious ministry in the Ottoman Empire that collects, reads, sorts and interprets the dreams of its citizens as a prophetic means of unlocking crimes and conspiracies against the state. Into this organization goes Mark-Alem, a maternal son of the powerful Quprili family.<br /><br />I reached a place in the story where I felt myself on the verge of a revelation, where the story was about unfold for me like an origami crane reversing, but now, with space and time, I am not so sure that anything miraculous was about to happen. <br /><br />I may have been taken in by the fact that I was reading a book about dreams, about interpreting the subconscious night droppings of minds, and that the book was a translation of a translation. Remove after remove after hyperreal remove -- both within <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/267147.Dream_Palace_of_the_Arabs_A_Generation_s_Odyssey" title="Dream Palace of the Arabs A Generation's Odyssey by Fouad Ajami" rel="nofollow noopener">The Palace of Dreams</a> and without -- had taken me so far away from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/130849.Ismail_Kadar_" title="Ismail Kadaré" rel="nofollow noopener">Ismail Kadaré</a>'s original intent that I imagined myself, impossibly, about to stumble upon some <i>truth</i> that was waiting there just for me, and tucked away in a corner of my mind I think I've dreamt what that truth must be.<br /><br />But I lost the book, and mow I don't think I want it back. I have a feeling the ending I imagined means more to me than the actual ending of ever could, and to read the last thirty-eight pages of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/130849.Ismail_Kadar_" title="Ismail Kadaré" rel="nofollow noopener">Kadaré</a>'s tale would taint my experience. So this mildly creepy, oddly fascinating book will remain (un)finished for me.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_229202766" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/229202766" />
    <a title="Lilla Smee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2869530-lilla-smee"><img alt="Lilla Smee" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1256598331p2/2869530.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/229202766?book_show_action=true">Oct 30, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lilla Smee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lilla Smee" href="/user/show/2869530-lilla-smee">Lilla Smee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099518273" href="/book/show/5978984-the-palace-of-dreams">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer229202766" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13790556157373487053">I could compare Kadare to both Kafka and Orwell (and I would not be the first to do so), as this novel has both the surreal, nightmarish quality of <i>The Trial</i> and the social and political weight of Orwell's <i>1984</i>. The novel allegorises a totalitarian police state though a bizarre, nightmarish beaurocracy in the form of the 'Tabir Sarrail' - the Palace of Dreams. The story is set in the 'United Ottoman States,' yet its culture and politics more closely resemble that of socialst eastern europe; ther</span>
  <span id="freeText13790556157373487053" style="display:none">I could compare Kadare to both Kafka and Orwell (and I would not be the first to do so), as this novel has both the surreal, nightmarish quality of <i>The Trial</i> and the social and political weight of Orwell's <i>1984</i>. The novel allegorises a totalitarian police state though a bizarre, nightmarish beaurocracy in the form of the 'Tabir Sarrail' - the Palace of Dreams. The story is set in the 'United Ottoman States,' yet its culture and politics more closely resemble that of socialst eastern europe; there is no sense of the exotic one might associate with the Ottoman empire. At the Palace, the dreams of each of the state's inhabitants are recorded, sorted, interpreted and analysed, with the purpose of identifying and anticipating any threats to the security of the vast empire. It's a horrifyingly extreme form of surveillance and control enforced by a state institution. <br /><br />The protagonist is Mark-Alem, and it is through his eyes that we are shown both the terror and the ridiculousness of the system. Mark-Alem gets lost countless times while working in the Palace, as he tries to navigate his way between the various departments and sections. '... it took him nearly half an hour to get to the basement. Now what? he wondered when he found himself alone in a long vaulted passage ... Whatever happened he was bound to find his way out, as he had done the other times. He was used to this kind of misadventure now. As he walked along he discovered that the circular passage was crossed by others of varying widths, but he didn’t dare go along any of them for fear of getting lost. After half an hour it seemed to him he was back where he started from.' And yet with infinite patience, or simply a lack of will to question the status quo, Mark-Alem progresses his career through the ranks of the palace. It is through Mark-Alem's career success that the tension in the novel is developed, as he has to balance his role as a functionary of the state against his desire to reconnect with his ethnic Albanian background.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13790556157373487053" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
